role="main">\n\t\t\t<a id="top"></a>\n\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div id="siteNotice" class="mw-body-content"><!-- CentralNotice --></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class="mw-indicators mw-body-content">\n</div>\n\t\t\t<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">List of retired South Pacific cyclone names</h1>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div id="contentSub"></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">\n\t\t\t\t\tJump to:\t\t\t\t\t<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, \t\t\t\t\t<a href="#p-search">search</a>\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Winston_2016-02-20_0130Z_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Winston_2016-02-20_0130Z_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Winston_2016-02-20_0130Z_%28cropped%29.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Winston_2016-02-20_0130Z_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Winston_2016-02-20_0130Z_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Winston_2016-02-20_0130Z_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Winston_2016-02-20_0130Z_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4590" data-file-height="6120" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Winston_2016-02-20_0130Z_(cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nSatellite image of <a href="/wiki/Cyclone_Winston" title="Cyclone Winston">Cyclone Winston</a>, the strongest recorded tropical cyclone in the Southern Hemisphere</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone" title="Tropical cyclone">Tropical cyclones</a> are non-<a href="/wiki/Weather_front" title="Weather front">frontal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Low-pressure_area" title="Low-pressure area">low pressure systems</a> that develop, within an environment of warm <a href="/wiki/Sea_surface_temperature" title="Sea surface temperature">sea surface temperatures</a> and little vertical <a href="/wiki/Wind_shear" title="Wind shear">wind shear</a> aloft.<sup id="cite_ref-SPAC_TCOP_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SPAC_TCOP-1">[1]</a></sup> Within the South Pacific, names are assigned from a <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_names#Southern_Pacific_Ocean_.28160.C2.B0E_.E2.80.93_120.C2.B0W.29" class="mw-redirect" title="Tropical cyclone names">pre-determined list</a>, to such systems, once they reach or exceed ten-minute <a href="/wiki/Maximum_sustained_wind" title="Maximum sustained wind">sustained wind speeds</a> of 65&#160;km/h (40&#160;mph), near the center, by either the <a href="/wiki/Fiji_Meteorological_Service" title="Fiji Meteorological Service">Fiji Meteorological Service</a> or <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/MetService" title="MetService">MetService</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-SPAC_TCOP_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SPAC_TCOP-1">[1]</a></sup> Within the South Pacific, tropical cyclones have been <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_naming" title="Tropical cyclone naming">officially named</a> since the <a href="/wiki/Pre-1970_Southern_Hemisphere_tropical_cyclone_seasons#1960.E2.80.9361_to_1968.E2.80.9369_cyclone_seasons" title="Pre-1970 Southern Hemisphere tropical cyclone seasons">1964\xe2\x80\x9365 South Pacific cyclone season</a>, though a few meteorological papers show that a few tropical cyclones were named before 1964\xe2\x80\x9365.<sup id="cite_ref-SPAC_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SPAC-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vanuatu_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vanuatu-4">[4]</a></sup> The names of significant tropical cyclones that have caused a high amount of damage and/or caused a significant number of deaths are retired from the lists of tropical cyclone names by the <a href="/wiki/World_Meteorological_Organization" title="World Meteorological Organization">World Meteorological Organization</a>\'s RA&#160;V Tropical Cyclone Committee at their bi-annual meeting.<sup id="cite_ref-SPAC_TCOP_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SPAC_TCOP-1">[1]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Within the South Pacific, there have been a total of 103&#160;tropical cyclone names retired, with the 1990s having the most retired tropical cyclone names. The most intense tropical cyclone to have its name retired was <a href="/wiki/Cyclone_Winston" title="Cyclone Winston">Winston</a>, which had an estimated peak pressure of 884&#160;hPa (26.1&#160;inHg). The deadliest tropical cyclone to have its name retired was Severe Tropical Cyclone Namu, which caused over 100 deaths, when it affected the Solomon Islands in May 1986. The most damaging system was <a href="/wiki/Cyclone_Yasi" title="Cyclone Yasi">Yasi</a> which caused over <span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7009250000000000000\xe2\x99\xa0</span>US$2.5&#160;billion in damage to Vanuatu, the Solomon Islands, and Australia in January and February 2011.</p>\n<p></p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc">\n<div class="toctitle">\n<h2>Contents</h2>\n</div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Background"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Background</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-2"><a href="#Tropical_cyclone_names_retired_in_the_1970s"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Tropical cyclone names retired in the 1970s</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-3"><a href="#Tropical_cyclone_names_retired_in_the_1980s"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Tropical cyclone names retired in the 1980s</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-4"><a href="#Tropical_cyclone_names_retired_in_the_1990s"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Tropical cyclone names retired in the 1990s</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-5"><a href="#Tropical_cyclone_names_retired_in_the_2000s"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Tropical cyclone names retired in the 2000s</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-6"><a href="#Tropical_cyclone_names_retired_in_the_2010s"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Tropical cyclone names retired in the 2010s</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-7"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-8"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-9"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-10"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<p></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_retired_South_Pacific_cyclone_names&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone_naming" title="Tropical cyclone naming">Tropical cyclone naming</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_historical_tropical_cyclone_names" class="mw-redirect" title="List of historical tropical cyclone names">List of historical tropical cyclone names</a>, and <a href="/wiki/History_of_tropical_cyclone_naming" title="History of tropical cyclone naming">History of tropical cyclone naming</a></div>\n<p>Within the region the credit for the first usage of personal names for weather systems, is generally given to the <a href="/wiki/Queensland_Government" class="mw-redirect" title="Queensland Government">Queensland Government</a> Meteorologist <a href="/wiki/Clement_Wragge" class="mw-redirect" title="Clement Wragge">Clement Wragge</a>, who named systems between 1887 \xe2\x80\x93 1907.<sup id="cite_ref-Whats_in_a_name.3F_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whats_in_a_name.3F-5">[5]</a></sup> Wragge used names drawn from the letters of the Greek alphabet, Greek and Roman mythology and female names, to describe weather systems over <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic" title="Antarctic">Antarctic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Whats_in_a_name.3F_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whats_in_a_name.3F-5">[5]</a></sup> After the new <a href="/wiki/Australian_government" class="mw-redirect" title="Australian government">Australian government</a> had failed to create a federal weather bureau and appoint him director, Wragge started naming cyclones after political figures.<sup id="cite_ref-TC_FAQ_B1_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TC_FAQ_B1-6">[6]</a></sup> This system of naming weather systems subsequently fell into disuse for several years after Wragge retired, until it was revived in the region, by the New Caledonia Meteorological Office during the <a href="/wiki/1958-59_South_Pacific_cyclone_season" class="mw-redirect" title="1958-59 South Pacific cyclone season">1958\xe2\x80\x9359 cyclone season</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Whats_in_a_name.3F_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whats_in_a_name.3F-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NZ_TC_39-69_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NZ_TC_39-69-7">[7]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/1963-64_Australian_region_cyclone_season" class="mw-redirect" title="1963-64 Australian region cyclone season">1963\xe2\x80\x9364 cyclone season</a> the <a href="/wiki/Australian_Bureau_of_Meteorology" class="mw-redirect" title="Australian Bureau of Meteorology">Australian Bureau of Meteorology</a> started to use female names for tropical cyclones that occurred within the Australian region, before the New Zealand Meteorological Service\'s Fiji office also started using female names for tropical cyclones within the South Pacific during the <a href="/wiki/1969-70_South_Pacific_cyclone_season" class="mw-redirect" title="1969-70 South Pacific cyclone season">1969\xe2\x80\x9370 cyclone season</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Whats_in_a_name.3F_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whats_in_a_name.3F-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NZ_TC_39-69_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NZ_TC_39-69-7">[7]</a></sup> During the International Women\'s Year of 1975 the NZMS decided to incorporate male names into the naming lists for the South Pacific, following a request from the Fiji National Council of Women who considered the practice discrimination.<sup id="cite_ref-Whats_in_a_name.3F_5-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whats_in_a_name.3F-5">[5]</a></sup> At around the same time the <a href="/wiki/Minister_for_Innovation,_Industry,_Science_and_Research_(Australia)#List_of_science_ministers" class="mw-redirect" title="Minister for Innovation, Industry, Science and Research (Australia)">Australian Science Minister</a> ordered that tropical cyclones, within the Australian region should carry both men\'s and women\'s names.<sup id="cite_ref-Whats_in_a_name.3F_5-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whats_in_a_name.3F-5">[5]</a></sup> This was because the minister thought "that both sexes should bear the odium of the devastation caused by cyclones."<sup id="cite_ref-Whats_in_a_name.3F_5-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whats_in_a_name.3F-5">[5]</a></sup> As a result, male names were added to the lists of names for both basins, ahead of the 1975\xe2\x80\x9376 season.<sup id="cite_ref-Whats_in_a_name.3F_5-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whats_in_a_name.3F-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Later that decade as the dual sex naming of tropical cyclones started in the Northern Hemisphere, the NZMS looked at adding ethnic Pacific names to the naming lists rather than the European names that were currently used.<sup id="cite_ref-Whats_in_a_name.3F_5-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whats_in_a_name.3F-5">[5]</a></sup> As a result of the many languages and cultures in the Pacific there was a lot of discussion surrounding this matter, with one name "Oni" being dropped as it meant the end of the world in one language.<sup id="cite_ref-Whats_in_a_name.3F_5-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whats_in_a_name.3F-5">[5]</a></sup> One proposal suggested that cyclones be named from the country nearest to which they formed, however, this was dropped when it was realized that a cyclone might be less destructive in its formative stage than later in its development.<sup id="cite_ref-Whats_in_a_name.3F_5-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whats_in_a_name.3F-5">[5]</a></sup> Eventually it was decided to throw names from all over the South Pacific into a pot at a training course, where each course member provided a list of names that were short, easily pronounced, culturally acceptable throughout the Pacific and did not contain any idiosyncrasies.<sup id="cite_ref-Whats_in_a_name.3F_5-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whats_in_a_name.3F-5">[5]</a></sup> These names were then collated, edited for suitability before being cross checked with the group for acceptability.<sup id="cite_ref-Whats_in_a_name.3F_5-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whats_in_a_name.3F-5">[5]</a></sup> It was intended that the four lists of names should be alphabetical with alternative male and female names while using only ethnic names, however it was not possible to complete the lists using only ethnic names so some European names were added in.<sup id="cite_ref-Whats_in_a_name.3F_5-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whats_in_a_name.3F-5">[5]</a></sup> As a result, there was a scattering of European names in the final naming lists, which have been used by the <a href="/wiki/Fiji_Meteorological_Service" title="Fiji Meteorological Service">Fiji Meteorological Service</a> and NZMS since the <a href="/wiki/1980-81_South_Pacific_cyclone_season" class="mw-redirect" title="1980-81 South Pacific cyclone season">1980\xe2\x80\x9381 season</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Whats_in_a_name.3F_5-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whats_in_a_name.3F-5">[5]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The practice of retiring significant names was started during 1955 by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Weather_Bureau" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Weather Bureau">United States Weather Bureau</a> in the Northern Atlantic basin, after hurricanes <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Carol" title="Hurricane Carol">Carol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Edna" title="Hurricane Edna">Edna</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Hazel" title="Hurricane Hazel">Hazel</a> struck the <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_United_States" title="Northeastern United States">Northeastern United States</a> and caused a significant amount of damage in the previous year.<sup id="cite_ref-Mahina_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mahina-9">[9]</a></sup> Initially the names were only designed to be retired for ten years after which they might be reintroduced, however, it was decided at the 1969 Interdepartmental hurricane conference, that any significant hurricane in the future would have its name permanently retired.<sup id="cite_ref-Mahina_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mahina-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Anna_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anna-10">[10]</a></sup> The first tropical cyclone name to be removed in the South Pacific, was Rosie after it had impacted Vanuatu and New Caledonia during 1971.<sup id="cite_ref-SPAC_TCOP_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SPAC_TCOP-1">[1]</a></sup> Several names have been removed from the Pacific naming lists for various other reasons than causing a significant amount of death/destruction, which include being pronounced in a very similar way to other names and political reasons.<sup id="cite_ref-WMO_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WMO-11">[11]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HRD_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HRD-12">[12]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tropical_cyclone_names_retired_in_the_1970s">Tropical cyclone names retired in the 1970s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_retired_South_Pacific_cyclone_names&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Tropical cyclone names retired in the 1970s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>During the 1970s, nineteen tropical cyclone names were retired from the list of names, with the 1971\xe2\x80\x9372 season having the most amount of retired tropical cyclone names. Severe Tropical Cyclone Carlotta, was the most intense tropical cyclone, during the decade with an estimated peak pressure of 940&#160;hPa (28&#160;inHg).</p>\n<table class="wikitable sortable 1980s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_retired_South_Pacific_cyclone_names&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Tropical cyclone names retired in the 1980s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>During the 1980s twenty-one tropical cyclone names were retired from the naming lists, with both the 1986\xe2\x80\x9387 and 1982\xe2\x80\x9383 season having the most amount of retired tropical cyclone names. The most intense tropical cyclone during the decade was <a href="/wiki/1982-83_South_Pacific_cyclone_season#Cyclone_Oscar" class="mw-redirect" title="1982-83 South Pacific cyclone season">Severe Tropical Cyclone Oscar</a> which had an estimated peak pressure of 920&#160;hPa (27.17&#160;inHg).</p>\n<table class="wikitable sortable plainrowheaders" style="margin:auto">\n<tr>\n<th class="sortbottom">\n<th colspan="6"><sup id="cite_ref-References_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-References-23">[nb 1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Intensity_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Intensity-25">[nb 2]</a></sup></th>\n<th></th>\n<th></th>\n<th></th>\n</tr>\n</table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tropical_cyclone_names_retired_in_the_1990s">Tropical cyclone names retired in the 1990s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_retired_South_Pacific_cyclone_names&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Tropical cyclone names retired in the 1990s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>During the 1990s twenty-six tropical cyclones names were retired from the naming lists, with the 1997\xe2\x80\x9398 season having the most amount of retired tropical cyclone names. The most intense tropical cyclones during the decade were Severe Tropical Cyclones Ron and Susan which had an estimated peak pressure of 900&#160;hPa (26.58&#160;inHg). Severe Tropical Cyclone Val was the most damaging system, which caused about <span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7008250000000000000\xe2\x99\xa0</span>250&#160;million&#160;(1992 USD) in damage to four different countries during December&#160;1991. The deadliest tropical cyclone was Severe Tropical Cyclone Martin which caused 28 deaths when it affected the Cook Islands in October and November 1997. Despite being retired the name Joni, was later reused during the <a href="/wiki/2008-09_South_Pacific_cyclone_season" class="mw-redirect" title="2008-09 South Pacific cyclone season">2008\xe2\x80\x9309 season</a>.</p>\n<table class="wikitable sortable plainrowheaders" style="margin:auto">\n<tr>\n<th scope="col">Name</th>\n<th scope="col">Dates active</th>\n<th id="cite_ref-Impact_53-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Impact-53">[51]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-1998-99_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1998-99-62">[60]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[61]</a></sup></td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Cyclone_Rona%E2%80%93Frank" title="Cyclone href="#cite_note-1998-99-62">[60]</a></sup></td>\n</tr>\n<tr class="sortbottom">\n<th colspan="6"><sup id="cite_ref-References_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-References-23">[nb 1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Intensity_25-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Intensity-25">[nb 2]</a></sup></th>\n<th></th>\n<th></th>\n<th></th>\n</tr>\n</table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tropical_cyclone_names_retired_in_the_2000s">Tropical cyclone names retired in the 2000s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_retired_South_Pacific_cyclone_names&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Tropical cyclone names retired in the 2000s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tropical_Cyclone_Heta_2004.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Tropical_Cyclone_Heta_2004.jpg/220px-Tropical_Cyclone_Heta_2004.jpg" width="220" height="289" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Tropical_Cyclone_Heta_2004.jpg/330px-Tropical_Cyclone_Heta_2004.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Tropical_Cyclone_Heta_2004.jpg/440px-Tropical_Cyclone_Heta_2004.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6400" data-file-height="8400" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tropical_Cyclone_Heta_2004.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nCyclone Heta at peak intensity on January 6, 2004</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>During the 2000s, 20 names of significant tropical cyclones were retired from the tropical cyclone list of names. Four names were retired after both the <a href="/wiki/2002%E2%80%9303_South_Pacific_cyclone_season" title="2002\xe2\x80\x9303 South Pacific cyclone season">2002\xe2\x80\x9303</a> and <a href="/wiki/2004%E2%80%9305_South_Pacific_cyclone_season" title="2004\xe2\x80\x9305 South Pacific cyclone season">2004\xe2\x80\x9305</a> seasons while the 2009\xe2\x80\x9310 season had five names retired. No names were retired from the naming lists, after the <a href="/wiki/2005%E2%80%9306_South_Pacific_cyclone_season" title="2005\xe2\x80\x9306 South Pacific cyclone season">2005\xe2\x80\x9306</a> and <a href="/wiki/2008%E2%80%9309_South_Pacific_cyclone_season" title="2008\xe2\x80\x9309 South Pacific cyclone season">2008\xe2\x80\x9309</a> seasons. <a href="/wiki/Cyclone_Zoe" title="Cyclone Zoe">Severe Tropical Cyclone Zoe</a> of 2002\xe2\x80\x9303 became the most intense tropical cyclone during the decade to have its name retired having reached an estimated peak pressure of 890&#160;hPa (26.28&#160;inHg). The deadliest tropical cyclone to have its name retired during the decade was <a href="/wiki/Cyclone_Ami" title="Cyclone Ami">Severe Tropical Cyclone Ami</a> which caused 14 deaths when it affected Fiji in January 2003, while the most damaging system was <a href="/wiki/Cyclone_Heta" title="Cyclone Heta">Severe Tropical Cyclone Heta</a> which caused over US$225&#160;million in damage to 7 different nations over the new year of <a href="/wiki/2003%E2%80%9304_South_Pacific_cyclone_season" title="2003\xe2\x80\x9304 South Pacific cyclone season">2003\xe2\x80\x9304</a>.</p>\n<div id="cite_ref-References_23-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-References-23">[nb 1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Intensity_25-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Intensity-25">[nb 2]</a></sup></th>\n<th></th>\n<th></th>\n<th></th>\n</tr>\n</table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tropical_cyclone_names_retired_in_the_2010s">Tropical cyclone names retired in the 2010s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_retired_South_Pacific_cyclone_names&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Tropical cyclone names retired in the 2010s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pam_2015-03-13_0220Z.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Pam_2015-03-13_0220Z.jpg/220px-Pam_2015-03-13_0220Z.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Pam_2015-03-13_0220Z.jpg/330px-Pam_2015-03-13_0220Z.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Pam_2015-03-13_0220Z.jpg/440px-Pam_2015-03-13_0220Z.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6300" data-file-height="8400" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pam_2015-03-13_0220Z.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nCyclone Pam near peak intensity on March 13, 2015</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>During the 2010s 15 tropical cyclone names have been retired, because of impacts caused to either Australia or various South Pacific Island Nations including Fiji, Vanuatu and French Polynesia. The most intense tropical cyclone to have its name retired during the decade was Severe Tropical Cyclone Winston, which was also the most intense of the Southern Hemisphere in history and had an estimated pressure of 884&#160;hPa (26.10&#160;inHg) at its peak intensity. The deadliest tropical cyclone to have its name retired was also Winston, which killed 44 people in Fiji in February 2016. The most damaging system during the decade was Severe Tropical Cyclone Yasi which caused over US$2.5 billion in damage to Vanuatu, Solomon Islands and Australia during January and February 2011.</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<table class="wikitable sortable plainrowheaders" style="margin:auto">\n<tr>\n<th scope="col">Name</th>\n<th scope="col">Dates active</th>\n<th
